Popperâs writings on logic were reviewed by prominent mathematical logicians, 
and many other logicians and philosophers knew his papers or were at least aware of 
their existence, as occasional references show. Nevertheless, the overall consideration 
of this work as a philosophically signif i cant contribution to formal logic and to the 
foundations of deductive reasoning was limited â something that remains the case 
today. To improve this situation, our idea was to produce an edition which allows the 
reader easy access to Popperâs logical works, which at the same time is suf i ciently 
comprehensive to cover most aspects of what we consider to be their essence. 
The central part of this collection are the six articles published in 1947â1949 on 
the foundations of deductive logic (Chapters 2â7). They are also the topic of our 
introduction to Popperâs inferentialist conception of logic (Chapter 1). These articles 
are accompanied by a short note of 1943 (Chapter 8). This note deals with negation in 
a manner that shows traces of what would be fully developed in the later articles, and 
which is interesting from the point of view of modern discussions on paraconsistent 
logics, the ex falso rule and disjunctive syllogism. These articles are furthermore 
accompanied by three later papers written in the 1950s, which exemplify that Popper 
continued to be interested in logic, although not primarily from the inferentialist 
perspective. Chapter 9 shows Popperâs abiding interest in Tarskiâs work, here in 
the def i nitions of satisfaction and truth in contradistinction to logical consequence, 
which is the basis of the inferentialist approach. Chapter 10 takes up a discussion 
of the liar paradox by Fitch, arguing that declaring a liar sentence to be neither 
true nor false does not escape paradoxicality. Chapter 11 discusses the subjunctive 
conditional, which plays a signif i cant role in philosophy of science when it comes 
to the meaning of natural laws. Chapter 12 is reproduced from Schilpp (1974) and 
presents Popperâs reply to Lejewski (1974), who had dealt with Popperâs inferential 
approach to logic. This reply contains many interesting comments on the background 
to Popperâs development of his inferentialist approach. In Chapter 13 we reprint the 
reviews of Popperâs articles on logic. These were written by renowned logicians, and 
some of them were very critical of Popperâs approach, both for conceptual reasons 
and for reasons of technical exposition. We doubt, however, that these reviews were 
the reason for the poor reception of Popperâs logical works then and now. Even the 
most critical of these reviews left open the possibility to pursue certain basics of 
Popperâs works in revised forms. We would rather claim that Popperâs inferentialism 
as well as his extensive discussion of dualities and non-classical negations (beyond 
just intuitionism) was too much ahead of its time. Inferentialism, proof-theoretic 
semantics and corresponding theories of meaning, apart from their implicit presence

His work in this area, developed primarily during his time in New Zealand (1937â1945) and later in London, is less known but holds substantial value both historically and systematically.Popper's perspective on logic can be described as "inferentialist," focusing on inferential definitions of logical signs. He dedicated a significant portion of his time post-1945 to formal logic, as evidenced by his enthusiastic series of articles from 1947 to 1949. This shift in focus came after the success of his political philosophy work, "The Open Society and Its Enemies." Despite his dedication and the potential for continued research in logic, the reception within the logic community at the time was limited.Popper's logical writings include a series of articles on the foundations of deductive logic and discussions on topics like paraconsistent logics, the liar paradox, and the subjunctive conditional. These writings, while reviewed by prominent logicians and known to others in the field, did not gain significant traction as major philosophical contributions to formal logic or deductive reasoning.The limited reception of Popper's work in logic might not be due to the critical nature of the reviews it received, but possibly because his ideas, including his inferentialism and discussions on non-classical negations, were ahead of their time. His works hold relevance for modern discussions in proof-theoretic semantics and theories of meaning.Overall, Karl Popper's contributions to deductive logic, though lesser-known, represent a significant, forward-thinking aspect of his philosophical legacy, meriting closer attention and recognition.Youin the work of Gentzen, became prominent only in the late 1960s in the aftermath of

The second part of this volume presents a number of manuscripts from Popperâs 
Nachlass, which are related to the published papers. Chapter 14 is a draft of a joint 
paper by Bernays and Popper that did not make it into a publication. Chapter 15 
discusses the relation between logical and descriptive signs and situates it in relation 
to Tarskiâs concept of logical consequence and to ideas by Carnap in his Introduction 
to Semantics (Carnap, 1942). Chapter 16 is an introduction to an intended study of 
classical and non-classical negations, which i gures prominently in Chapters 5 and 6. 
Chapter 17 is a somewhat later draft from 1952 which tries to derive truth tables from 
inferential characterizations. Chapter 18 deals with the distinction between derivation 
and demonstration, a topic that according to our assessment Popper would have 
pursued more deeply had he continued to systematically publish in logic. Chapters 19 
and 20 are lecture notes (from 1939â1941) and general considerations on the origins 
of modern logic, respectively. These texts put modern formal logic in a broader 
philosophical perspective and elucidate how Popper saw logic as a discipline. We 
here present only a selection of these notes, which we consider to be representative 
of Popperâs views. There are considerably more manuscripts in his Nachlass, though 
many of them are variants of what is published here. 
In the third part we reproduce letters to and from Popper dealing with his logical 
works. As with the second part, this is a selection of items based on what we consider 
best suited to situate Popperâs logical work and its background in the context of 
his interactions with colleagues. For somebody who wants to study sophisticated 
historical issues, letters exchanged with other authors might be relevant, many of 
which are available in the Karl Popper Collection Klagenfurt and at the Hoover 
Institution Library & Archives, Stanford.5 
We decided to omit from this collection materials that deal with one nonetheless 
important theme, namely Popperâs work on Boolean algebra. Popper had studied 
Boolean algebra already before he moved on to the inferential approach to logic 
presented here, and studied and discussed it for the rest of his career, combining 
purely algebraic (lattice-theoretic) investigations with his attempts to axiomatize

Popperâs Theory of Deductive Logic 
David Binder, Thomas Piecha, and Peter Schroeder-Heister 
Abstract We present Popperâs theory of deductive logic as exhibited in his articles 
published between 1947 and 1949. After an introduction to Popperâs inferentialist 
approach and his idea of âinferential def i nitionsâ of logical constants, we discuss in 
more formal detail Popperâs general theory of the deducibility relation (which, using 
Gentzenâs terminology, might be called his âstructuralâ theory), as well as his special 
theoryoflogicalconstants.Weputspecialemphasisonhisinferentialnotionofduality, 
which includes his analysis of the âanti-conditionalâ (today called âco-implicationâ), 
his systematicstudy ofvarious forms ofnegation including classical,intuitionisticand 
weaker negations, his system of bi-intuitionistic logic, and his theory of quantif i cation 
and identity. We also touch on his treatment of modal operators, which is based on 
Carnapâs Meaning and Necessity. 
Key words: Karl Popper, logic, inferentialism, logical constant, deducibility, nega- 
tion, duality, co-implication, intuitionistic logic, bi-intuitionistic logic, quantif i cation, 
identity, modal operators

objections raised by contemporary reviewers, who were quite prominent i gures

spite of the fact that their author was a well-known i gure, although not in logic, but in 
both philosophy of science (Logik der Forschung) and social and political philosophy

Was it Popperâs proof-theoretic framework, which he considered suf i cient to lay the 
foundations for logic, independent of any additional sort of semantics? There were

from todayâs perspective we would call âinferentialistâ,1as did Brouwer, Kneale and

From a modern standpoint, where inferentialism and proof-theoretic semantics 
have become respected foundational approaches, Popperâs views look quite advanced. 
Perhaps Popper was, with his inferentialist perspective, too much ahead of his time, 
and did not come back to his logical investigations, when in the spirit of a re-appraisal 
of Gentzenâs work foundational proof-theoretic approaches started to l ourish. Formal 
logic is not what one normally associates with Popper, and there were so many 
important debates in philosophy of science in the late 1960s in which Popper was 
involved, such that contributing to the debate on the foundational role of proof theory 
(Dummett, 1975; Kreisel, 1971; Prawitz, 1971) was way beyond his interests at that 
time. 
A particular role seems to have been played by Tarski, whom Popper admired, 
but whose semantical approach was incompatible with the inferentialism Popper 
put forward in his logical papers. Popper wrote in a letter of 9 July 1982 (this 
volume, Â§32.2): âI was discouraged at the time by the fact that Alfred Tarski, whom I 
admire very much, did not want to have a look at these works. I had no one elseâ.2

well be imagined that in spite of the fact that his logical papers were written âwith 
much enthusiasmâ (Popper, 1974b, p. 1095, this volume, Chapter 12, p. 217), he

authority and reputation he had acquired in the philosophy of science.

This chapter focuses on Popper's inferentialist approach to logic, which he developed in a series of articles published between 1947 and 1949. The key concepts and elements discussed in this chapter include:Inferential Definitions of Logical Constants: Exploring Popper's idea of defining logical constants through inference.General Theory of Deducibility: An examination of what could be termed, using Gentzenâs terminology, Popper's "structural" theory of logic.Special Theory of Logical Constants: A detailed look at Popper's specific theories regarding logical constants.Inferential Notion of Duality: This includes an analysis of the "anti-conditional" (known today as "co-implication").Study of Various Forms of Negation: Discussing classical, intuitionistic, and weaker forms of negation within Popper's framework.Bi-Intuitionistic Logic: Popper's system of bi-intuitionistic logic, including his theory of quantification and identity.Modal Operators: Touching on Popper's treatment of modal operators, based on Carnapâs "Meaning and Necessity".The chapter also addresses the historical context and reception of Popper's work. Despite being published in accessible journals and reviewed by prominent figures in mathematical logic, Popper's logical theories did not receive widespread attention at the time. This lack of reception is explored, considering potential reasons such as Popper's proof-theoretic framework and his bold claims like "new foundations" and "trivialization" of logic.From a modern perspective, Popper's views appear advanced, aligning with contemporary inferentialism and proof-theoretic semantics. GyËozËo I. Egri a, ZoltÂ´an Fodor abc, Christian Hoelbling b,

Abstract
The speed, bandwidth and cost characteristics of todayâs PC graphics cards make them an at-
tractive target as general purpose computational platforms. High performance can be achieved also
for lattice simulations but the actual implementation can be cumbersome. This paper outlines the
architecture and programming model of modern graphics cards for the lattice practitioner with the
goal of exploiting these chips for Monte Carlo simulations. Sample code is also given.
1
Introduction
The goal of every lattice ï¬eld theorist is to use a calculational platform that maximizes the perfor-
mance/price ratio. In this paper a competitive but so far unused and unappreciated (at least in the
lattice community) architecture will be introduced.
So far the only available option was the usage of CPU-based platforms may it be individual PCs, PC

3 MIN READ

The Video Game Engine in Your Head
Scientists use video games to explain how the brain interprets the world around us. 
BY JOSHUA HARTSHORNE

Mind & Brain
For years now, physicists and engineers have been building computer simulations of physics in order to understand the behavior of objects in the world. Want to see if a bridge would be stable during an earthquake? Enter it into the simulation, apply earthquake dynamics, and see what happens.

Recently, the prestigious Proceedings of the National Academy of Sciences published work by MIT psychologists (and my labmates) Peter Battaglia, Jessica Hamrick, and Joshua Tenenbaum, arguing that all humans do roughly the same thing when trying to understand or make predictions about the physical world. The primary difference is that we run our simulations in our brains rather than in digital computers, but the basic algorithms are roughly equivalent. The analogy runs deep: To model human reasoning about the physical world, the researchers actually used an open-source computer game physics engine â the software that applies the laws of physics to objects in video games in order to make them interact realistically (think Angry Birds). 

Battaglia and colleagues found that their video game-based computer model matches human physical reasoning far better than any previous theory. The authors asked people to make a number of predictions about the physical world: will tower of blocks stand or fall over, what direction would it fall over, and where would the block that landed the farthest away land; which object would most likely fall off of a table if the table was bumped; and so on. In each case, human judgments closely matched the prediction of the computer simulation ... but not necessarily the actual world, which is where it gets interesting.

Whether or not a tower of blocks (or a bridge, etc.) will fall over depends on its exact dimensions and the exact position of each block, potentially down to the millimeter. But a human can't tell the dimensions and position of every block down to the millimeter just by looking. That is why we invented rulers. Instead, we know approximately how big each block is and where it is. If the simulation was given the exact coordinates of the blocks, it predicted human judgments reasonably well but far from perfectly. If the simulation was given approximate coordinates, taking into account human uncertainty, it matched the actual world less well but human judgments very well.

In retrospect, it may seem intuitive that when we made predictions about the physical world â what will happen to towers during earthquakes or books on shelves â we query an internal, virtual simulation of the real world, but it represents a sharp departure from previous scientific thinking. Many scientists thought that we use rules-of-thumb to predict the world around us.
 
As a theory, the rules-of-thumb account seems to be a failure: Battaglia and colleagues tested a number of plausible rules-of-thumb. For instance, maybe we base our guesses as to whether a block tower will fall based on the towerâs height or center of mass. None of the rules-of-thumb fared as well as the simulation account. Even worse, rules-of-thumb have to be tailored to the question: The heuristics for the tower of blocks scenario are useless for the table-bump scenario. In contrast, simulations are one-size-fits-all.

But that leaves the question of whether the simulation account is plausible. After all, if we already have a physics simulator in our heads, why did scientists have to discover the laws of physics, and why do we have to learn physics in school (or not at all)? Part of the answer may lie in the distinction between implicit and explicit knowledge. A bird doesn't have to be able to teach a university course on aerodynamics in order to fly, and we don't have to understand biophysics in order to walk or neuroscience in order to thinkChatGPTChatGPTThe article "The Video Game Engine in Your Head" by Joshua Hartshorne discusses a fascinating concept: that our brains function similarly to a video game engine when interpreting and predicting physical phenomena in the world around us. This idea is based on research published in the Proceedings of the National Academy of Sciences by MIT psychologists Peter Battaglia, Jessica Hamrick, and Joshua Tenenbaum, Hartshorne's labmates.Key points from the article:Simulation in the Brain: The researchers propose that the human brain runs simulations to understand and predict the physical world, much like computer simulations are used in physics and engineering. Our brains employ similar basic algorithms to those in digital computers but execute these simulations internally.Using a Game Physics Engine: To model human reasoning about the physical world, the researchers utilized an open-source computer game physics engine. This software, which applies physical laws in video games for realistic interactions, was used to emulate how humans might predict physical events.Human Judgments and Computer Models: The study found that human predictions about physical events (like the stability of a block tower or objects falling off a table) closely matched the outcomes predicted by the video game-based computer model. Interestingly, these predictions aligned with the model's results more than the actual physical world, especially when the model was given approximate coordinates to mimic human uncertainty.The Limitations of Rule-of-Thumb: The research contrasts the simulation approach with the previously thought rules-of-thumb method. Rules-of-thumb, or heuristics, are specific to particular scenarios and less effective compared to the more versatile and comprehensive simulation approach.Implicit vs. Explicit Knowledge: The article concludes with a reflection on why we need to learn physics explicitly if our brains inherently run physics simulations. The distinction lies in implicit knowledge (like a bird flying without understanding aerodynamics) versus explicit, taught knowledge. Our internal simulations help us navigate the physical world, but they don't equate to a conscious understanding of physics principles.This research suggests that our brains are equipped with an innate capability to simulate and predict physical phenomena, a process similar to the algorithms used in video game physics engines. This perspective offers a novel understanding of human cognition and its relationship with the physical world.YouPhysics doesn't actually have to simulate a game engine, the building blocks of the universe perform automatic calculations on their own substrate by performing local energy exchangeChatGPTChatGPTYour perspective offers an intriguing view on how the fundamental processes of the universe operate. This method can add a layer of abstraction and flexibility to your code. Here's a closer look at the concept:Abstraction and Flexibility: By using a function to return a constant value, you introduce a level of abstraction. Even though the function currently returns a fixed value, its implementation can be modified later without changing the places in the code where the function is called. This allows for greater flexibility if you decide to change how the value is determined or if the value needs to be generated dynamically in the future.Encapsulation and Control: This approach can also encapsulate the logic for determining the value. For instance, if the value needs to be calculated or retrieved from an external source, the function can be modified to include this logic. This keeps the implementation details hidden and maintains a clean interface.Maintainability and Scalability: In larger, more complex systems, using functions for values can enhance maintainability. If the logic for determining the value becomes complex, having it in one place (the function) rather than scattered throughout the code makes it easier to manage and scale.Consistency and Error Reduction: When you use a function, you ensure that every part of your program that needs this value calls the same function. This uniformity reduces the risk of errors, such as accidentally using different values in different parts of the program.Potential Performance Considerations: For simple constants, using a function might add a negligible overhead compared to using a direct constant variable. However, in most high-level programming languages, this overhead is minimal and often outweighed by the benefits of maintainability and flexibility.In summary, while using a function to return a constant value may seem like an additional step compared to directly using a constant variable, it can provide significant benefits in terms of code flexibility, maintainability, and future-proofing your codebase.YouI was thinking of how in Python the existence of an object is seen as a test of its truthiness, so you could say if toggle-set:

Python indeed has a very flexible and intuitive way of evaluating the truth value of objects in conditional statements like if. This behavior is deeply integrated into Python's design philosophy, which often emphasizes readability and simplicity.In Python, most objects are considered "truthy," meaning they are considered true in a boolean context, with some exceptions like None, False, 0, and empty collections ([], {}, (), '', etc.), which are considered "falsy." Here's a closer look at your example and how Python's polymorphism and truthiness evaluation play a role:Implicit 'then' and 'return': In Python, the if statement implicitly checks for the truthiness of the expression that follows it. If the expression evaluates to True (or a truthy value), the indented block of code under the if statement is executed. There's no need for an explicit 'then' keyword, which is common in some other programming languages.Polymorphic Typing: Python is dynamically typed, meaning variables don't have fixed types. You can assign any type of object to a variable, and Python will interpret it correctly at runtime. This allows for flexibility in how variables are used and what they can represent.Example Usage: In your example, toggle-set (though typically Python variable names would use underscores, like toggle_set) can be assigned different types of values, such as a boolean (True or False), an integer, a string, etc. Depending on its value, the if toggle-set: statement will behave differently:If toggle-set is True (or a truthy value like 5, "text", [1,2,3], etc.), the code inside the if block will execute.If toggle-set is False (or a falsy value like False, None, 0, "", [], etc.), the code inside the if block will not execute.Evaluating Objects: When you check if toggle-set:, Python evaluates the truthiness of the object referred to by toggle-set. If the object exists and is not a falsy value, the statement evaluates to True, and the subsequent block is executed.This aspect of Python makes for very readable and concise code. It allows for easy checking of variables without needing to explicitly compare them to True or False, or check if they are None, empty, etc. This feature is extensively used in Pythonic code for its simplicity and effectiveness.YouOk it's a reasonable explanation, but I am looking to illustrate the topics we were discussing earlier. nionChatGPTChatGPTThe article "No belief propagation required: Belief space planning in high-dimensional state spaces via factor graphs, the matrix determinant lemma, and re-use of calculation" by Dmitry Kopitkov and Vadim Indelman, published in the International Journal of Robotics Research, introduces a computationally efficient approach for belief space planning (BSP) in robotics and artificial intelligence.Here are the key points:Belief Space Planning (BSP): BSP is fundamental in robotics and AI, with applications in autonomous driving, surveillance, sensor deployment, object manipulation, and active simultaneous localization and mapping (SLAM). BSP involves decision-making under uncertainty, where the goal is to determine the best actions based on a specified objective function, considering the current belief about random variables of interest (like robot poses or mapped environments).Handling Uncertainty: Since the true state is often unknown and only partially observable through measurements, it's represented through a probability distribution conditioned on available data. BSP approaches consider how this distribution evolves due to candidate actions and expected observations.Challenge of High-Dimensional State Spaces: Traditional BSP methods involve propagating belief states for each candidate action by calculating the posterior information (or covariance) matrix and then computing its determinant (for entropy). This process can be computationally intensive, especially in high-dimensional spaces.New Approach: The authors propose a new method that avoids the runtime complexity of traditional belief propagation. They utilize factor graphs and an augmented version of the matrix determinant lemma, allowing for a one-time calculation dependent on state dimensionality and per-candidate calculations independent of this dimensionality.Reuse of Calculation: A significant aspect of their approach is the reuse of calculations when evaluating the impact of different candidate actions. This efficiency is crucial in making the approach more computationally feasible than existing methods.Applications and Performance: The proposed method is applicable to various scenarios, including autonomous navigation in unknown environments and sensor deployment. The authors demonstrate through simulations and real-world data that their approach significantly reduces computation time without compromising performance.Addressing Computational Challenges: By focusing on sub-optimal approaches that balance optimality and runtime complexity, the article addresses the computational challenges in BSP, particularly the curse of history and dimensionality, common in solving partially observable Markov decision processes (POMDP).In summary, Kopitkov and Indelman present an innovative approach to BSP that is computationally efficient and effective for high-dimensional state spaces, a significant contribution to the field of robotics and AI.YouExplain this a bit more: They utilize factor graphs and an augmented version of the matrix determinant lemma, allowing for a one-time calculation dependent on state dimensionality and per-candidate calculations independent of this dimensionality.

In the context of belief space planning (BSP), factor graphs are employed to represent the relationships and dependencies among various random variables (like robot positions, sensor measurements, etc.) and the factors that affect these variables. This graphical representation helps in simplifying and structuring the complex probabilistic models used in BSP.Augmented Matrix Determinant Lemma: The matrix determinant lemma is a mathematical tool used to compute the determinant of a matrix after it has been modified. In BSP, determining the determinant of matrices is crucial for understanding the entropy (or uncertainty) of a system. The authors have augmented this lemma to facilitate calculations in BSP. This augmentation likely makes the lemma more suitable for the high-dimensional matrices that are typical in BSP problems.One-time Calculation Dependent on State Dimensionality: The approach includes a one-time, upfront computation that is dependent on the dimensionality of the state space (i.e., the number of variables and their possible states in the BSP problem). This calculation likely involves setting up the factor graph and computing certain properties that remain constant throughout the decision-making process, regardless of the specific actions being considered.Per-candidate Calculations Independent of State Dimensionality: For each potential action (candidate action) that the system might take, additional calculations are required. However, these calculations are designed to be independent of the state space's dimensionality. This means that the complexity of these calculations does not increase as the number of variables or their potential states increases, which is a significant advantage in high-dimensional problems.Reuse of Calculation: An important innovation in their approach is the ability to reuse certain calculations when evaluating different candidate actions. In traditional methods, each potential action might require recalculating many aspects of the BSP problem from scratch. By reusing calculations, the authorsâ method reduces the overall computational load. This reuse is possible due to the way the factor graph is structured and the augmented matrix determinant lemma is applied.In essence, these aspects of Kopitkov and Indelman's approach significantly improve the efficiency of belief space planning in robotics and AI. By structuring the problem using factor graphs, enhancing mathematical tools like the matrix determinant lemma, and designing the process to reuse calculations, the method can handle high-dimensional state spaces more effectively than traditional methods. - Belief space planning (BSP) and decision making under uncertainty involve evaluating candidate actions based on an objective function that accounts for uncertainty. Computing this objective function, which often involves entropy or mutual information terms, is computationally challenging in high-dimensional state spaces. 

- State-of-the-art approaches calculate the posterior belief and objective function from scratch for each candidate action, leading to high computational cost that increases with state dimensionality.

- The paper proposes a new approach that avoids recomputing the posterior belief for each action. It formulates the problem using factor graphs and develops an augmented version of the matrix determinant lemma. 

- This allows the impact of candidate actions to be evaluated with complexity independent of state dimensionality. Computations can also be reused across candidate actions, reducing computational cost significantly.

- The approach handles both fixed-state and augmented-state problems, applicable to domains like active SLAM, sensor deployment, etc. It also extends to the "focused" case where uncertainty is reduced over a subset of variables only.

- The key innovations are the factor graph representation, augmented matrix determinant lemma, and algorithms for reusing computations across actions. This makes BSP and decision making computationally efficient even in high-dimensional state spaces.

- The approach is evaluated on simulation examples and real data, showing significant speedups compared to state-of-the-art methods without loss of performance.

- Jacobian matrices of candidate actions capture how actions influence future beliefs, avoiding explicit belief propagation.  

- Calculations can be reused for different actions, and approaches are applicable to general observation/motion models.

- Solutions are presented for non-augmented BSP, augmented BSP, and focused BSP problems like SLAM, sensor deployment, graph reduction.

- Experimental validation compares the proposed approaches to state-of-the-art in terms of runtime for various scenarios.

Transformers are able to learn long-range dependencies in text, which makes them well-suited for tasks such as machine translation, text summarization, and question answering.
One of the challenges of using transformers is that they can require a lot of memory and processing power, especially when using large context windows. Sharding is a technique that can be used to address this challenge.
Sharding is a technique that divides a large dataset or model into smaller, more manageable chunks. This can be useful for training and using machine learning models, especially for models that require a lot of memory or processing power.
There are a number of different ways to shard a transformer for large context windows. One approach is to divide the context window into multiple smaller windows, each of which is processed on a different node. The nodes then communicate with each other to exchange information about the hidden states of their submodels.
Another approach to sharding a transformer for large context windows is to use a technique called parameter sharding. In parameter sharding, the weights of the transformer are divided into multiple shards, each of which is stored on a different node. The nodes then communicate with each other to update the weights of the transformer.
Sharding can offer a number of advantages for large context windows. First, sharding can allow the transformer to be trained and used on much larger datasets than would be possible with a centralized transformer. Second, sharding can improve the performance of the transformer by reducing the amount of communication required between nodes. Third, sharding can make the transformer more robust to failures of individual nodes.
However, sharding also introduces some challenges. One challenge is that it can be more difficult to implement and debug sharded models than centralized models. Another challenge is that sharded models can be more sensitive to communication delays and errors.
Overall, sharding is a powerful technique that can be used to train and use transformers with large context windows. However, it is important to be aware of the challenges involved in implementing and using sharded models.
Here are some examples of when sharding might be used for transformers with large context windows:
When training a machine translation model on a very large dataset of text and translations.
When using a speech recognition model to generate real-time transcripts of spoken language.
When using a question answering model to answer questions in a comprehensive and informative way.
In general, sharding should be used for transformers with large context windows only when the benefits of distribution outweigh the challenges of implementation and debugging.
Here are some additional considerations for using sharding for transformers with large context windows:
The type of sharding: There are a number of different ways to shard a transformer for large context windows. The best approach will depend on the specific task that the transformer is being used for and the specific resources that are available.
The communication overhead: Sharding can introduce some communication overhead, as the nodes need to communicate with each other to exchange information. This overhead can be reduced by using efficient communication protocols and by carefully designing the sharding scheme.
The robustness to failures: Sharding can make the transformer more robust to failures of individual nodes. However, it is important to carefully design the sharding scheme to ensure that the transformer can continue to operate even if one or more nodes fail.
Overall, sharding is a powerful technique that can be used to train and use transformers with large context windows. However, it is important to be aware of the challenges involved in implementing and using sharded models.

Retentive Network: A Successor to Transformer for Large Language Models
Yutao Sun, Li Dong, Shaohan Huang, Shuming Ma, Yuqing Xia, Jilong Xue, Jianyong Wang, Furu Wei
Download PDF
In this work, we propose Retentive Network (RetNet) as a foundation architecture for large language models, simultaneously achieving training parallelism, low-cost inference, and good performance. We theoretically derive the connection between recurrence and attention. Then we propose the retention mechanism for sequence modeling, which supports three computation paradigms, i.e., parallel, recurrent, and chunkwise recurrent. Specifically, the parallel representation allows for training parallelism. The recurrent representation enables low-cost O(1) inference, which improves decoding throughput, latency, and GPU memory without sacrificing performance. The chunkwise recurrent representation facilitates efficient long-sequence modeling with linear complexity, where each chunk is encoded parallelly while recurrently summarizing the chunks. Experimental results on language modeling show that RetNet achieves favorable scaling results, parallel training, low-cost deployment, and efficient inference. The intriguing properties make RetNet a strong successor to Transformer for large language modelsChatGPTChatGPTThe paper introduces the Retentive Network (RetNet), proposed as a successor to the Transformer architecture for large language models. Key points include:Foundation Architecture for Large Language Models: RetNet is designed as a foundational architecture, aiming to address some limitations of Transformer models while maintaining or improving upon their performance.Theoretical Connection Between Recurrence and Attention: The authors establish a theoretical link between recurrent neural network mechanisms and attention mechanisms, which are central to Transformer models.Retention Mechanism for Sequence Modeling: RetNet introduces a retention mechanism that supports three computation paradigms:Parallel Computation: Enables training parallelism, allowing for efficient processing and learning.Recurrent Computation: Facilitates low-cost O(1) inference, which improves decoding throughput, latency, and GPU memory efficiency without degrading performance.Chunkwise Recurrent Computation: Addresses efficient long-sequence modeling with linear complexity, where individual chunks are processed in parallel but are also summarized recurrently.Experimental Results: RetNet has been tested in language modeling tasks, demonstrating favorable scaling results. The experiments highlight RetNet's capabilities in parallel training, low-cost deployment, and efficient inference.Advantages of RetNet: The architecture combines the strengths of both parallel and recurrent computations, leading to a system that is efficient both in terms of training and inference, especially for large-scale models.RetNet as a Successor to Transformer: The paper positions RetNet as a strong successor to the Transformer architecture, particularly for large language models. Its design addresses some of the Transformer's limitations, particularly in handling long sequences and computational efficiency.In summary, RetNet is presented as an innovative architecture that could potentially redefine the landscape of large language models by providing a more efficient and scalable alternative to the Transformer model, particularly in scenarios requiring parallelism and long-sequence processing.YouIsn't ret Latin for net?ChatGPTChatGPTThe word "ret" itself is not Latin for "net." On the Expressive Power of Geometric Graph Neural Networks
Chaitanya K. Joshi, Cristian Bodnar, Simon V. Mathis, Taco Cohen, Pietro LiÃ²
Download PDF
The expressive power of Graph Neural Networks (GNNs) has been studied extensively through the Weisfeiler-Leman (WL) graph isomorphism test. However, standard GNNs and the WL framework are inapplicable for geometric graphs embedded in Euclidean space, such as biomolecules, materials, and other physical systems. In this work, we propose a geometric version of the WL test (GWL) for discriminating geometric graphs while respecting the underlying physical symmetries: permutations, rotation, reflection, and translation. We use GWL to characterise the expressive power of geometric GNNs that are invariant or equivariant to physical symmetries in terms of distinguishing geometric graphs. GWL unpacks how key design choices influence geometric GNN expressivity: (1) Invariant layers have limited expressivity as they cannot distinguish one-hop identical geometric graphs; (2) Equivariant layers distinguish a larger class of graphs by propagating geometric information beyond local neighbourhoods; (3) Higher order tensors and scalarisation enable maximally powerful geometric GNNs; and (4) GWL's discrimination-based perspective is equivalent to universal approximation.ChatGPTChatGPTThe paper "On the Expressive Power of Geometric Graph Neural Networks" by Chaitanya K. Joshi et al. contributes significantly to the field of Graph Neural Networks (GNNs), particularly in understanding and enhancing their applicability to geometric graphs. Let's connect this with our previous discussions:Geometric Graphs and High-Dimensional Data:Challenges in Geometric Graphs: The paper addresses the challenges of applying standard GNNs to geometric graphs that are embedded in Euclidean space, such as biomolecules and physical systems. This is similar to the challenges discussed in handling high-dimensional data in large language models and lattice QCD simulations. Both scenarios require innovative approaches to efficiently process complex, structured data.Popper's Logical Structures: The approach to enhancing GNNs' ability to process geometric graphs can be seen as an application of systematic and structured problem-solving, resonating with Karl Popper's emphasis on structured approaches in logic.Enhancing Computational Models:Geometric WL Test: The introduction of a geometric version of the Weisfeiler-Leman (WL) test (GWL) to evaluate GNNs' capabilities parallels the development of RetNet and sharding techniques in Transformers. Each represents an advancement in computational models to better handle specific types of data or structures.Physical Symmetries in AI: The consideration of physical symmetries (permutations, rotation, reflection, translation) in the GWL test aligns with the concept of incorporating real-world phenomena into AI models, much like how the human brain's processing (discussed in the context of the brain functioning like a video game engine) and RetNet handle complex, sequential information.Improving Expressivity and Efficiency:Geometric GNNs: The paper's focus on characterizing the expressive power of geometric GNNs, especially in terms of their invariance or equivariance to physical symmetries, mirrors the goal in AI and machine learning to develop models that are both expressive and computationally efficient.RetNet and Geometric GNNs: Both RetNet and the advancements in geometric GNNs are about pushing the boundaries of what neural network architectures can achieve, whether itâs in language processing or in analyzing complex geometric data.Impact of Design Choices:Design Choices in Geometric GNNs: The paper's analysis of how design choices (like invariant layers, equivariant layers, higher-order tensors, scalarisation) influence the expressivity of geometric GNNs provides insights that are valuable across AI applications. This includes understanding how different architectural choices impact the performance and capabilities of models like RetNet or sharded Transformers.In summary, "On the Expressive Power of Geometric Graph Neural Networks" adds to the broader discussion of advancing neural network architectures and computational techniques to effectively handle complex, structured, and high-dimensional data. The development of GWL and the exploration of geometric GNNs' expressivity contribute to the ongoing evolution of AI models, paralleling advancements in language models, lattice QCD simulations, and understanding of human cognition.YouI did a green marker drawing October 19, 2021 of a dot-to-dot transformer robot coming apart into a graph network with connected nodes and edges and the caption. Chaitanya K. Joshi
Transformers are Graph Neural Networks
Exploring the connection between Transformer models such as GPT and BERT for Natural Language Processing, and Graph Neural Networks.

Chaitanya K. Joshi
Last updated on Jun 21, 2021 12 min read
      
Photo by Arseny Togulev
My engineering friends often ask me: deep learning on graphs sounds great, but are there any real applications or big commercial success stories?

While Graph Neural Networks are used in recommendation systems at at Pinterest, Alibaba and Twitter, a more subtle success story is the Transformer architecture, which has taken the NLP industry by storm. Through this post, I want to establish a link between Graph Neural Networks (GNNs) and Transformers. Iâll talk about the intuitions behind model architectures in the NLP and GNN communities, make connections using equations and figures, and discuss how we can work together to drive future progress.

Letâs start by talking about the purpose of model architectures â representation learning.

Representation Learning for NLP
At a high level, all neural network architectures build representations of input data as vectors/embeddings, which encode useful statistical and semantic information about the data. These latent or hidden representations can then be used for performing something useful, such as classifying an image or translating a sentence. The neural network learns to build better-and-better representations by receiving feedback, usually via error/loss functions.

For Natural Language Processing (NLP), conventionally, Recurrent Neural Networks (RNNs) build representations of each word in a sentence in a sequential manner, i.e., one word at a time. Intuitively, we can imagine an RNN layer as a conveyor belt, with the words being processed on it autoregressively from left to right. In the end, we get a hidden feature for each word in the sentence, which we pass to the next RNN layer or use for our NLP tasks of choice.

I highly recommend Chris Olahâs legendary blog for recaps on RNNs and representation learning for NLP.


Initially introduced for machine translation, Transformers have gradually replaced RNNs in mainstream NLP. The architecture takes a fresh approach to representation learning: Doing away with recurrence entirely, Transformers build features of each word using an attention mechanism to figure out how important all the other words in the sentence are w.r.t. to the aforementioned word. Knowing this, the wordâs updated features are simply the sum of linear transformations of the features of all the words, weighted by their importance.

Back in 2017, this idea sounded very radical, because the NLP community was so used to the sequentialâone-word-at-a-timeâstyle of processing text with RNNs. The title of the paper probably added fuel to the fire! For a recap, Yannic Kilcher made an excellent video overview.

Breaking down the Transformer
Letâs develop intuitions about the architecture by translating the previous paragraph into the language of mathematical symbols and vectors. We update the hidden feature 
 of the 
âth word in a sentence 
 from layer 
 to layer 
 as follows:


 


where 
 denotes the set of words in the sentence and 
 are learnable linear weights denoting the Query, Key and Value for the attention computation, respectively. The attention mechanism is performed parallelly for each word in the sentence to obtain their updated features in one shot â another plus point for Transformers over RNNs, which update features word-by-word.

We can understand the attention mechanism better through the following pipeline:


Taking in the features of the word 
 and the set of other words in the sentence 
, we compute the attention weights 
 for each pair 
 through the dot-product, followed by a softmax across all 
âs. Finally, we produce the updated word feature 
 for word 
 by summing over all 
âs weighted by their corresponding 
. Each word in the sentence parallelly undergoes the same pipeline to update its features.

Multi-head Attention mechanism
Getting this dot-product attention mechanism to work proves to be tricky. Bad random initializations can de-stabilize the learning process. We can overcome this by parallelly performing multiple âheadsâ of attention and concatenating the result, with each head now having separate learnable weights:


where 
 are the learnable weights of the 
âth attention head and 
 is a down-projection to match the dimensions of 
 and 
 across layers.

Multiple heads allow the attention mechanism to essentially âhedge its betsâ, looking at different transformations or aspects of the hidden features from the previous layer. Weâll talk more about this later.

Scale issues and the Feed-forward sub-layer
A key issue motivating the final Transformer architecture is that the features for words after the attention mechanism might be at different scales or magnitudes: (1) This can be due to some words having very sharp or very distributed attention weights 
 when summing over the features of the other words. (2) Additionally, at the individual feature/vector entries level, concatenating across multiple attention headsâeach of which might output values at different scalesâcan lead to the entries of the final vector 
 having a wide range of values. Following conventional ML wisdom, it seems reasonable to add a normalization layer into the pipeline.

Transformers overcome issue (2) with LayerNorm, which normalizes and learns an affine transformation at the feature level. Additionally, scaling the dot-product attention by the square-root of the feature dimension helps counteract issue (1).

Finally, the authors propose another âtrickâ to control the scale issue: a position-wise 2-layer MLP with a special structure. After the multi-head attention, they project 
 to a (absurdly) higher dimension by a learnable weight, where it undergoes the ReLU non-linearity, and is then projected back to its original dimension followed by another normalization:


To be honest, Iâm not sure what the exact intuition behind the over-parameterized feed-forward sub-layer was. I suppose LayerNorm and scaled dot-products didnât completely solve the issues highlighted, so the big MLP is a sort of hack to re-scale the feature vectors independently of each other. According to Jannes Muenchmeyer, the feed-forward sub-layer ensures that the Transformer is a universal approximator. Thus, projecting to a very high dimensional space, applying a non-linearity, and re-projecting to the original dimension allows the model to represent more functions than maintaining the same dimension across the hidden layer would.

The final picture of a Transformer layer looks like this:


The Transformer architecture is also extremely amenable to very deep networks, enabling the NLP community to scale up in terms of both model parameters and, by extension, data. Residual connections between the inputs and outputs of each multi-head attention sub-layer and the feed-forward sub-layer are key for stacking Transformer layers (but omitted from the diagram for clarity).

GNNs build representations of graphs
Letâs take a step away from NLP for a moment.

Graph Neural Networks (GNNs) or Graph Convolutional Networks (GCNs) build representations of nodes and edges in graph data. They do so through neighbourhood aggregation or message passing, where each node gathers features from its neighbours to update its representation of the local graph structure around it. Stacking several GNN layers enables the model to propagate each nodeâs features over the entire graphâfrom its neighbours to the neighboursâ neighbours, and so on.

Take the example of this emoji social network: The node features produced by the GNN can be used for predictive tasks such as identifying the most influential members or proposing potential connections.
Take the example of this emoji social network: The node features produced by the GNN can be used for predictive tasks such as identifying the most influential members or proposing potential connections.
In their most basic form, GNNs update the hidden features 
 of node 
 (for example, ð) at layer 
 via a non-linear transformation of the nodeâs own features 
 added to the aggregation of features 
 from each neighbouring node 
:

 

where 
 are learnable weight matrices of the GNN layer and 
 is a non-linearity such as ReLU. In the example, 
(ð) 
 { ð, ð, ð, ð¤© }.

The summation over the neighbourhood nodes 
 can be replaced by other input size-invariant aggregation functions such as simple mean/max or something more powerful, such as a weighted sum via an attention mechanism.

Does that sound familiar?

Maybe a pipeline will help make the connection:


If we were to do multiple parallel heads of neighbourhood aggregation and replace summation over the neighbours 
 with the attention mechanism, i.e., a weighted sum, weâd get the Graph Attention Network (GAT). Add normalization and the feed-forward MLP, and voila, we have a Graph Transformer!
Sentences are fully-connected word graphs
To make the connection more explicit, consider a sentence as a fully-connected graph, where each word is connected to every other word. Now, we can use a GNN to build features for each node (word) in the graph (sentence), which we can then perform NLP tasks with.


Broadly, this is what Transformers are doing: they are GNNs with multi-head attention as the neighbourhood aggregation function. Whereas standard GNNs aggregate features from their local neighbourhood nodes 
, Transformers for NLP treat the entire sentence 
 as the local neighbourhood, aggregating features from each word 
 at each layer.

Importantly, various problem-specific tricksâsuch as position encodings, causal/masked aggregation, learning rate schedules and extensive pre-trainingâare essential for the success of Transformers but seldom seem in the GNN community. At the same time, looking at Transformers from a GNN perspective could inspire us to get rid of a lot of the bells and whistles in the architecture.

What can we learn from each other?
Now that weâve established a connection between Transformers and GNNs, let me throw some ideas aroundâ¦

Are sentences fully-connected graphs?
Before statistical NLP and ML, linguists like Noam Chomsky focused on developing fomal theories of linguistic structure, such as syntax trees/graphs. Tree LSTMs already tried this, but maybe Transformers/GNNs are better architectures for bringing the world of linguistic theory and statistical NLP closer? For example, a very recent work from MILA and Stanford explores augmenting pre-trained Transformers such as BERT with syntax trees [Sachan et al., 2020].1 / 2ChatGPTChatGPTHere is a visual representation of the concept "Transformers - Graph Neural Networks (in disguise)" that merges the idea of a transformer robot with the structure of a graph network.YouNice. Abstract 
We introduce Episodic Memory QA, the 
task of answering personal user questions 
grounded on memory graph (MG), where 
episodic memories and related entity nodes 
are connected via relational edges. We cre- 
ate a new benchmark dataset i rst by gen- 
erating synthetic memory graphs with simu- 
lated attributes, and by composing 100K QA 
pairs for the generated MG with bootstrapped 
scripts.To address the unique challenges 
for the proposed task, we propose Memory 
Graph Networks (MGN), a novel extension of 
memory networks to enable dynamic expan- 
sion of memory slots through graph traver- 
sals, thus able to answer queries in which con- 
texts from multiple linked episodes and exter-

the Episodic Memory QA Net with multiple 
module networks to effectively handle various 
question types.Empirical results show im- 
provement over the QA baselines in top-k an- 
swer prediction accuracy in the proposed task. 
The proposed model also generates a graph 
walk path and attention vectors for each pre- 
dicted answer, providing a natural way to ex- 
plain its QA reasoning.

ChatGPTChatGPTThe concept of "Retentive Graph Networks" connects well with the paper "Memory Graph Networks for Explainable Memory-grounded Question Answering" by Seungwhan Moon et al. from Facebook Assistant. Both concepts involve using graph structures to improve the memory and explainability of neural network models. Let's explore the similarities and connections:Memory Grounding in Graph Structures:Both "Retentive Graph Networks" and "Memory Graph Networks" imply that a graph structure is used to represent and retain information. This can be episodic memories in the case of the Facebook Assistant paper or long-range dependencies in text for language models.Graph Traversals for Information Retrieval:Memory Graph Networks (MGN) use graph traversal to dynamically expand memory slots and access relevant information for answering questions. The idea of "Retentive Graph Networks" could similarly involve traversing through stored information in a structured manner to retain and utilize knowledge over time.Explainability Through Graph Paths:MGNs provide a natural way to explain their reasoning by generating a graph walk path and attention vectors for each predicted answer. The notion of "Retentive Graph Networks" would also benefit from such explainability features, as understanding the network's decision-making process is crucial, especially in complex tasks like language understanding.Handling Complex Data Structures:Just as Memory Graph Networks are designed to handle complex queries based on a user's memory graph, "Retentive Graph Networks" could be conceptualized to manage complex data structures, such as the interdependencies in large language models.Dynamic Memory and Computation:MGNs propose a dynamic memory expansion mechanism through graph traversals. Similarly, "Retentive Graph Networks" could incorporate mechanisms for dynamic memory retention, possibly drawing on the strengths of both Transformers and GNNs.In summary, "Retentive Graph Networks" as a concept encapsulates the ideas of using structured graph representations to encode, retrieve, and reason with large amounts of information dynamically. This concept aligns with the work presented in the Facebook Assistant paper, which uses Memory Graph Networks to answer personal user questions grounded in memory graphs, showcasing a method for neural networks to retain and process information efficiently and explainably.YouSee if you can give an overview of Retentive Graph Networks, assuming they incorporate ideas from the rest of this conversation.ChatGPTChatGPT"Retentive Graph Networks" (RGNs) could be an overarching term for a class of neural network architectures that combine the principles of graph neural networks (GNNs) with memory retention capabilities inspired by the fields of NLP and logical inference. Drawing from the breadth of our conversation, here's an overview of what RGNs might encompass:Graph-Based Structure:Like GNNs, RGNs would leverage the power of graph-based data structures to represent complex relationships within data. Nodes in these graphs could represent entities or concepts, while edges would capture the interactions or dependencies between them.Memory Retention:Inspired by memory networks and Retentive Networks, RGNs would integrate mechanisms to retain and recall information over time, akin to episodic memory in humans. This feature would be crucial for tasks that require an understanding of context or history, such as question answering based on past interactions.Scalability and Efficiency:Drawing on ideas from sharding and advancements in transformers, RGNs would be designed for scalability, allowing them to handle large datasets and high-dimensional state spaces efficiently. This could involve techniques to parallelize computation and distribute the model's parameters across multiple processing units.Explainability:Following the lead of Memory Graph Networks, RGNs would incorporate explainability into their core, providing insights into the reasoning behind their outputs. This could be achieved through mechanisms that track and visualize the graph traversal paths taken during the decision-making process.Attention and Recurrence:RGNs would integrate attention mechanisms to selectively focus on relevant parts of the graph structure. They might also include recurrent aspects to process sequential data or to capture dynamic changes in the graph over time.Physical and Logical Consistency:In line with the geometric version of the Weisfeiler-Leman test, RGNs would ensure consistency with underlying physical symmetries such as rotation and translation, making them applicable to domains like molecular biology where spatial relationships are key.The models would also reflect logical structures, potentially drawing from Karl Popper's contributions to logic, to ensure that inference and reasoning tasks are grounded in robust logical foundations.Complexity Management:To manage complexity, RGNs would likely adopt advanced techniques such as those found in RetNet, such as chunkwise processing for long sequences, ensuring efficient computation without compromising performance.In essence, Retentive Graph Networks would be a synthesis of multiple AI disciplines, aiming to create a versatile and powerful tool for modeling complex systems that evolve over time and require a nuanced understanding of a vast web of relationships. This would make them suitable for a wide range of applications, from personal digital assistants to advanced scientific modeling tools.YouHow does it tie into some of my other research?
